        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.9b06760"><meta property="og:title" content="Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/acsami.9b06760.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="We study the stability of several diglyme-based electrolytes in sodium|sodium and sodium|graphite cells. The electrolyte behavior for different conductive salts [sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf), NaPF6, NaClO4, bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI), and sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI)] is compared and, in some cases, considerable differences are identified. Side reactions are studied with a variety of methods, including X-ray diffraction, scanning electron microscopy, transmission electron microscopy, online electrochemical mass spectrometry, and in situ electrochemical dilatometry. For Na|Na symmetric cells as well as for Na|graphite cells, we find that NaOTf and NaPF6 are the preferred salts followed by NaClO4 and NaFSI, as the latter two lead to more side reactions and increasing impedance. NaTFSI shows the worst performance leading to poor Coulombic efficiency and cycle life. In this case, excessive side reactions lead also to a strong increase in electrode thickness during cycling. In a qualitative order, the suitability of the conductive salts can be ranked as follows: NaOTf ≥ NaPF6 > NaClO4 > NaFSI ≫ NaTFSI. Our results also explain two recent, seemingly conflicting findings on the degree of solid electrolyte interphase formation on graphite electrodes in sodium-ion batteries [Maibach, J.;  ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces 2017, 9, 12373−12381; Goktas, M.;  Adv. Energy Mater. 2018, 8, 1702724]. The contradictory findings are due to the different conductive salts used in both studies."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/acsami.9b06760.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931889919b1054b8-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931889919b1054b8-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '03a6d6fa-07ee-4b64-b229-784f74f4bd6f'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931889919b1054b8-MAN">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="931889919b1054b8-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Stable and Unstable Diglyme-Based Electrolytes for Batteries with Sodium or Graphite as Electrode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b06760", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">95</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0013.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">We study the stability of several diglyme-based electrolytes in sodium|sodium and sodium|graphite cells. The electrolyte behavior for different conductive salts [sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf), NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI), and sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI)] is compared and, in some cases, considerable differences are identified. Side reactions are studied with a variety of methods, including X-ray diffraction, scanning electron microscopy, transmission electron microscopy, online electrochemical mass spectrometry, and in situ electrochemical dilatometry. For Na|Na symmetric cells as well as for Na|graphite cells, we find that NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub> are the preferred salts followed by NaClO<sub>4</sub> and NaFSI, as the latter two lead to more side reactions and increasing impedance. NaTFSI shows the worst performance leading to poor Coulombic efficiency and cycle life. In this case, excessive side reactions lead also to a strong increase in electrode thickness during cycling. In a qualitative order, the suitability of the conductive salts can be ranked as follows: NaOTf ≥ NaPF<sub>6</sub> &gt; NaClO<sub>4</sub> &gt; NaFSI ≫ NaTFSI. Our results also explain two recent, seemingly conflicting findings on the degree of solid electrolyte interphase formation on graphite electrodes in sodium-ion batteries [<contrib-group><span class="NLM_string-name">Maibach, J.</span></contrib-group>;   <cite><i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i></cite> <span class="NLM_year">2017</span>, <em>9</em>, 12373−12381; <contrib-group><span class="NLM_string-name">Goktas, M.</span></contrib-group>;   <cite><i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i></cite> <span class="NLM_year">2018</span>, <em>8</em>, <elocation-id>1702724</elocation-id>]. The contradictory findings are due to the different conductive salts used in both studies.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D32844%26pageCount%3D12%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DMustafa%2BGoktas%252C%2BChristoph%2BBolli%252C%2BJohannes%2BBuchheim%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D36%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b06760%26title%3DStable%2Band%2BUnstable%2BDiglyme-Based%2BElectrolytes%2Bfor%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BSodium%2Bor%2BGraphite%2Bas%2BElectrode%26numPages%3D12%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D32855%26publicationDate%3DSeptember%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291442&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Anode materials"> Anode materials </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon"> Carbon </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes"> Electrolytes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291301&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Salts"> Salts </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Diglyme-based+Electrolytes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_diglyme-based%20electrolytes">diglyme-based electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=OEMS&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_OEMS">OEMS</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Dilatometry&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_dilatometry">dilatometry</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Conductive+Salts&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_conductive%20salts">conductive salts</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) appear as viable option for meeting the rapidly growing market of rechargeable batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 1 to 8">(1−8)</a> Different from lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) that show the highest energy densities, SIBs aim at lowering costs while still providing a reasonably high energy density. To have an impact, developing SIB technology requires not only to “simply” replace lithium by sodium but also using other abundant elements. Despite their conceptual similarity, peculiar differences can be found when comparing LIBs and SIBs (or Li/air vs Na/air or Li/S vs Na/S) batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> A very notable exception is graphite. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Although being the commonly used negative electrode in LIBs, graphite is largely inactive in SIBs when using the conventional carbonate-based electrolytes. This limitation can be overcome by using solvent co-intercalation phenomena, i.e., solvated sodium ions are intercalated. This way, graphite shows a surprisingly reversible charge storage for sodium ions as initially shown by Jache and Adelhelm <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and Kim et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> using diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (diglyme, 2G) as a solvent. An interesting feature of this reaction is that the redox potential of the reaction can be tuned by a few hundred millivolts by using different types of glymes (mono- to tetraglyme). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> Very recently, pentaglyme and a crown ether have also been utilized by slightly increasing the cell temperature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> In the same study, it was also found that for mono-, di-, and tetraglyme, the reaction at room temperature thermodynamically proceeds with a temperature coefficient of about −2.55 ± 0.3 mV K<sup>–1</sup>. The high rate capability and the long cycle life (high Coulombic efficiency) are two other important characteristics of this reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 13 to 15 and 17 to 20">(13−15,17−20)</a> For example, Cohn et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> reported 8000 cycles and high rate performance up to 30 A g<sup>–1</sup> and Zhu et al. achieved up to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 6000 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> In an own study, we found values close to 80 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 11 A g<sup>–1</sup> corresponding to a C-rate of 100 (mass loading of 2–3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Very recently, Xu et al. have demonstrated the use of a graphite co-intercalation electrode over 1000 cycles in a full cell using Na<sub>1.5</sub>VPO<sub>4.8</sub>F<sub>0.7</sub> as cathode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> An overview on the recent literature is summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. The high rate capability has been rationalized by NMR measurements and theory. A diffusion coefficient of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> = 1.1 × 10<sup>–8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> was determined by Jung et al., which is almost one order of magnitude larger than the diffusion coefficient of Li<sup>+</sup> in LiC<sub>6</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The main conceptual question, however, relates to the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), which is commonly expected to form on the graphite surface. As intercalation of the solvated ions takes place below 1.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, the common understanding is that, depending on the used solvent and salt, SEI formation needs to take place due to the instability of liquid electrolytes at low potentials. On the other hand, an SEI would prevent the co-intercalation of solvent molecules, therefore preventing any intercalation of solvated ions. Moreover, the large expansion/shrinkage of the electrode during cycling should lead to a continuous rupture of the SEI combined with continuous electrolyte decomposition.</div><div class="NLM_p">Studies on SEI formation are notoriously difficult; nevertheless, in a recent study, we provided reasonable evidence for that the graphite/electrolyte interface is “SEI-free” and that the electrolyte is kinetically stable in contact with the graphite electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> On the other hand, Maibach et al. reported that SEI formation takes place. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> It is of note, however, that different electrolytes were used in both studies. While we used sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (sodium triflate, NaOTf) in diglyme, Maibach et al. used sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) dissolved in tetraglyme. As excellent cycle life has been reported for both solvents (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), we suggest that the type of conductive salt is responsible for the different observations made. So far, a number of salts (NaOTf, NaFSI, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaI) have been applied for the solvent co-intercalation reaction with graphite. For example, Jache and Adelhelm <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> compared NaI and NaOTf; Kim et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> compared NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, and NaOTf; and Cabello et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> compared NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf. Larger differences in the electrochemical performance were not found for the tested salts; however, none of the studies specifically focused on the stability of the salts.</div><div class="NLM_p">Westman et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> provided a detailed study on the stability of NaPF<sub>6</sub>/diglyme as electrolyte against hard carbon, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>. Experimental results indicated a generally sufficient stability of the electrolyte between 0.0 and 4.4 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, but poor SEI formation was found in the case of the hard carbon. Related to sodium metal as electrode, the same authors suggested on the basis of density functional theory calculations that the electrolyte is thermodynamically stable in contact with sodium metal. On the other hand, Tobishima et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> found that reductive decomposition of diglyme (with Pt as working electrode) occurs below about 0.4 V versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li. Related to Na–O<sub>2</sub> batteries, Lutz et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> studied the stability of monoglyme-based electrolytes against sodium metal and found a strong influence of the conductive salt on the SEI formation. The smallest impedance was obtained for NaPF<sub>6</sub> followed by NaOTf. The worst result with respect to the impedance was found for sodium bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI). This is in line with earlier findings by Seh et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> that identified NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme as preferred electrolyte for sodium metal electrodes. Related to ionic liquids, Hosokawa et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> found that bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (FSI) anions are preferred over bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (TFSI) anions in contact with sodium metal.</div><div class="NLM_p">Taking these findings and the unclear role of the SEI together into account, there is still very limited understanding on the role of the salt anion on the stability of diglyme-based electrolytes, especially with respect to intercalation electrodes. No systematic study exists so far for graphite as electrode in SIBs. A method that is particularly useful for studying the stability of electrolyte solutions is online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS). For the first time, we systematically apply this method to determine which salt anion is the best and which causes detrimental side reactions with graphite and sodium.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, we report on the influence of the salt anion on the stability and reversibility of solvent co-intercalation reactions with graphite. Diglyme is chosen as solvent because it so far provides the best results (lowest overpotentials) among all glymes used. Experiments are done in half-cells with sodium as counter electrode, which is why we also study the stability of the same electrolytes with sodium metal. For the salts, we compare NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaClO<sub>4</sub>, NaFSI, and NaTFSI. The concentration of the electrolyte solution is 1 M in all cases. From galvanostatic cycling, one can easily see side reactions occurring for NaTFSI and NaFSI, whereas much better stability is found for the other salts. We clarify these observations by using different analytical tools and reference experiments. We first study the general stability of sodium metal in the electrolyte solutions. For this, we conduct plating/stripping experiments in Na|Na symmetrical cells combined with electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS), and X-ray diffraction (XRD). After this, we study side reactions between the graphite electrode and the different electrolytes in graphite–sodium half-cells. For this, we use transmission electron microscopy (TEM), scanning electron microscopy (SEM), OEMS, and in situ electrochemical dilatometry.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a> show results from galvanostatic cycling of graphite in half-cells with sodium metal as the counter electrode. The corresponding voltage profiles are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. For NaOTf/diglyme as electrolyte, the initial sodiation capacity and the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) are 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 92.4%, respectively, which is in line with previous findings. For comparison, the capacity in carbonate electrolytes is negligible, pointing out the special role of the electrolyte solvent (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The losses in the initial cycle can be expected due to activation of the graphite electrode <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> and (if occurring) SEI formation. The voltage profile shows a staged behavior well in line with many previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref24 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 13 to 15, 24 and 33 to 35">(13−15,24,33−35)</a> Cycle life and Coulombic efficiency are also excellent for this electrolyte. Considering NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the ICE value is lower reaching 85%. The reversible capacity is also reduced; however, the overall stability is almost equally good compared to when NaOTf is used. In the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the ICE value is only 77%, indicating more side reactions. Compared to NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the cycle life is slightly poorer, leading to a gradual decrease in capacity. The use of NaFSI and NaTFSI leads to very low ICE values. Poor Coulombic efficiency and cycle life are found for these salts. Compared to the other salts, unusually high sodiation capacities indicate that reductive side reactions take place. These seem highly detrimental to cycle life. The most drastic capacity loss is observed for NaTFSI for which the graphite becomes largely inactive after 30–50 cycles.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Voltage profiles (1st and 10th cycles) of graphite–sodium cells with diglyme-based electrolytes for different electrolyte salts and (b) differential capacity plots. The measurements are performed at C/10 = 11 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (1 C corresponds to 110 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>(diglyme)<sub>2</sub>C<sub class="stack">20</sub><sup class="stack">–</sup>) within a voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Cycle life of graphite–sodium cells with diglyme-based electrolytes for different electrolyte salts and (b) Coulombic efficiency. The measurements are performed at C/10 = 11 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (1 C corresponds to 110 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>(diglyme)<sub>2</sub>C<sub class="stack">20</sub><sup class="stack">–</sup>) within a voltage window of 0.01–2.5 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For NaTFSI, the voltage profiles during desodiation are rather erratic, an indication for a very unstable electrode reaction. The derivative plots emphasize the clear difference for the different salts. Maxima in this plot indicate voltage plateaus, i.e., redox activity at a defined redox potential. While plots for the 1st and 10th cycles largely match in the case of NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the behavior is different for NaFSI and NaTFSI. For NaFSI, the first maximum during sodiation is less defined and is shifted to slightly lower values, peaking at 0.57 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The peak maximum for desodiation is also much broader. During cycling, a new reductive peak at 0.82 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na appears. The clear deviations compared to the much more defined behavior for NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaClO<sub>4</sub> can be taken as signs for side reactions. Peak shifting and broadening is also observed for NaTFSI. Here, one can also note an increase in overpotentials over cycling. One can also note an additional maximum at around 0.41 V (marked with an asterisk) that is not visible for the other salts. This also indicates a reductive instability of the electrolyte. A rate capability test for the different salts is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. In line with previous findings, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref17 ref18 ref20 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref17 ref18 ref20 ref25" aria-label="reference 14, 17, 18, 20 and 25">(14,17,18,20,25)</a> the electrodes can easily withstand currents as high as 6.6 A g<sup>–1</sup> (60 C), for example. In the case of NaTFSI, however, the performance is quite poor. The best performance was found for NaPF<sub>6</sub>, which might be due to the higher electrolyte conductivity obtained for this salt compared to NaOTf. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">In the following sections, we take a closer look at where the side reactions occur for the different electrolytes. For this, we need to separately consider the compatibility of the electrolyte against the graphite working electrode and the sodium metal counter electrode.</div><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Side Reactions with Sodium Metal</h3><div class="NLM_p">Sodium metal was exposed to the different electrolyte solutions (and to pure diglyme as reference) for several days. The metallic nature of sodium was preserved in most cases with NaTFSI being the exception. For this salt, rapid degradation of the sodium metal along with a color change took place. Images can be found in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4 and S5</a>). A slight color change was also observed in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>. Similar observations have been reported in the literature <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 and 32">(30,32)</a> although the obvious chemical instability is still surprising considering the frequent use of LiTFSI as salt in lithium-ion cells. We therefore also tested the stability of 1 M LiTFSI/diglyme electrolyte in contact with sodium metal and lithium metal. The comparison is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. While a good stability was found for Li/LiTFSI, corrosion still took place in the case of Na/LiTFSI. This shows that the instability seems intrinsic to the combination of sodium metal with TFSI<sup>–</sup> and is caused by, e.g., impurities (note that only lithium salts are commercially available in “battery grade”).</div><div class="NLM_p">While these results easily point on issues related to NaTFSI as salt, it does not mean that the other salts are stable during plating/stripping in an electrochemical cell. We therefore assembled symmetrical Na|Na cells and conducted galvanostatic plating/stripping experiments at ±9 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>. Results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b. Each current pulse was applied for 120 min, corresponding to plating/stripping of an ideally 0.16 μm thick layer of sodium. Clear differences between the different salts can be seen. The largest overpotentials are found for NaFSI with about 13 mV at ±9 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>. This value remains constant upon cycling, indicating a stable but, compared to the other salts, more resistive SEI. The smallest polarization is found for NaOTf followed by NaPF<sub>6</sub>. Consecutive cycling does not lead to any increase in resistance, indicating a rather stable and low-resistive SEI. For NaTFSI and NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the situation is different. Starting with relatively low polarization, the plating/stripping overpotential increases during cycling, which is a clear sign for poor surface film formation and continuous electrolyte decomposition.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Sodium plating/stripping measurements using Na|Na symmetric cells (9 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>). (b) Impedance data for Na/Na symmetrical cells. Cells were allowed to equilibrate for 48 h (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A more quantitative analysis was done by using electrochemical impedance spectroscopy in the frequency range of 1 MHz to 10 mHz. The data can be interpreted by making use of a simple equivalent circuit model, which is commonly accepted for analogue Li batteries <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>). Usually, this model consists of two parallel <i>R</i>/CPE units representing the charge transfer through the electrochemical double layer (<i>R</i><sub>CT</sub>/CPE<sub>dl</sub>) and the surface layer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>/CPE<sub>SEI</sub>). A single resistance in series (<i>R</i><sub>el</sub>) largely represents the bulk electrolyte, whereas possible diffusion limitations at low frequencies are described by a Warburg element (<i>W</i>).<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b displays the impedance spectra for symmetric cells obtained for the different salts. As assigned by Zhao et al., <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> we also attribute the higher-frequency response to surface layer impedance and lower-frequency response to charge transfer kinetics. Note that the values refer to the areal resistance.</div><div class="NLM_p">Results reveal a major impact of the salt anion on the electrode/electrolyte interface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7, and Table S2</a>). Note that an equilibration time of 48 h was given to reach stable impedance data (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>).<named-content content-type="anchor" rid="fig3" type="simple"></named-content> Electrolytes with NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaFSI show good ionic conductivity; however, choosing NaFSI leads to a rapid buildup of a highly resistive interface (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> = 1094 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 1567 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>). Much smaller values were recorded for NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>: 0.9 and 5.9 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> for <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>, and 0.3 and 6.5 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> for <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, respectively. Therefore, these electrolytes form a less resistive surface layer. Results for NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes are in accordance with the study of Westman et al., <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> i.e., the spectrum depicts a similar semicircle, and values for the resistances are small. For NaTFSI, the interface properties were also quite poor (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> ≈ 694 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> ≈ 600 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>) but, more importantly, a continuous increase of electrolyte resistance up to 287 Ω cm<sup>2</sup> emerged, indicating that strong side reactions are affecting the bulk phase liquid electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). This indicates a very unstable situation due to continuous corrosion. An intermediate behavior was found in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, i.e., the behavior was better compared to NaTFSI and NaFSI but still worse than in the case of NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>. It is of note, however, that the poor behavior for NaTFSI can be partly mitigated by adding NaPF<sub>6</sub> as co-salt (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>). For a 1:1 mixture, the sum over all interfacial resistances (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) after 24 h was only around 30 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>. This is only slightly higher compared to using NaPF<sub>6</sub> alone (12 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>) but much smaller compared to NaTFSI (1378 Ω cm<sup>2</sup>). While mixing conductive salts is known to be an effective strategy for tailoring electrolyte properties, a more detailed analysis on the specific situation for NaTFSI in contact with Na has to be part of future studies.</div><div class="NLM_p">An immediate question is how these findings compare to electrolyte solutions based on the much more common carbonate solvents. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b,c</a> shows time-dependent impedance data for electrolytes based on ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate and propylene carbonate (using NaPF<sub>6</sub> as salt). Results were in the range of several thousand ohms being more than 2 orders of magnitude higher compared to NaPF<sub>6</sub> or NaOTf in diglyme. The high values for carbonate-based electrolytes are well in line with a former study by Iermakova et al. who investigated the influence of different carbonate combinations on Li/electrolyte and Na/electrolyte interfaces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The poor stability of sodium metal in contact with several carbonate based electrolytes was also recently systematically studied by Pfeifer et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref200" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref200" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Overall, this clearly shows the favorable properties of diglyme as electrolyte solvent in contact with sodium metal (as long as not NaFSI or NaTFSI are used as salts).</div><div class="NLM_p">Side reactions between the different electrolyte solutions and sodium metal were also followed using online electrochemical mass spectrometry (OEMS) (see also <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> for more experimental details). Again, symmetrical Na|Na cells were used for this purpose and plating/stripping was done for three consecutive cycles at a current density of 110 μA cm<sup>–2</sup> with intervals of 9.5 h. The ion currents for fragments <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 2 (H<sub>2</sub>), 16 (CH<sub>4</sub>), 28 (CO), and 27 (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>) were recorded without calibration and converted into approximate gas evolution rates that allow direct semiquantitative comparison between the evolution of these fragments from one data set to another. Due to the absence of any evolution maxima for CO<sub>2</sub> (<i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 44; cf. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>), the ion fragment <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 28 is assigned only to CO. The ion current for fragments <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 27 (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>) was calibrated and the data allow quantitative comparison. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a–d shows the total amount of gas released during plating/stripping over three cycles for H<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>, CO, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>. H<sub>2</sub> is released in all cases, but the differences between the samples are rather small. In the case of NaFSI, NaOTf, and NaPF<sub>6</sub>, the main release occurs directly after the circuit is closed after idling at OCV (open-circuit voltage) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S10 and S11</a>) and is likely due the reduction of residual water impurities. For NaTFSI and NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the H<sub>2</sub> formation occurs during stripping/plating and is therefore related to electrolyte side reactions. For methane, the by far largest release is detected for NaTFSI, which amounts to about 16.1 μmol cm<sup>–2</sup>. The release of CO and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> is largest for NaTFSI and NaClO<sub>4</sub>. Taking these results together, a qualitative sequence for the degree of side reactions would be NaTFSI &gt; NaClO<sub>4</sub> ≫ (NaFSI, NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>). The larger the gas release, the poorer the stability of the electrolyte solution under plating/stripping conditions (note that pressure analysis also suggest only minor decomposition reactions in case of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> These results are well in line with the plating/stripping experiments shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b that show a gradual increase in resistance over time. In the case of NaTFSI, the side reactions come along with the formation of larger amounts of methane. For NaFSI, the gas release is moderate considering the large overpotentials required for plating and stripping. This indicates a thin but highly resistive SEI.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Total gas amount for (a) H<sub>2</sub>, (b) CH<sub>4</sub>, (c) CO, and (d) C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> for different electrolytes during cycling in Na|Na cells. The gas amount was calculated from the first three plating/stripping cycles and is normalized with respect to the surface area of the copper mesh on which Na was plated. The measurement signals over cycling are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Although OEMS is a very powerful tool for evidencing side reactions during cell cycling and it reveals dramatic differences between the different salts, it is important to remember that it does detect neither solid nor soluble decomposition products. These are, however, difficult to identify as disassembling of the cell is required (post mortem analysis), which easily causes secondary side reactions. Moreover, the total amount of decomposition products related to SEI/surface film formation is generally very small, which complicates the analysis. We nevertheless applied Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy and recorded several spectra of a NaTFSI electrolyte that was exposed to sodium metal up to 21 days. Results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) FTIR measurements for diglyme and for the different electrolyte solutions and (b) spectra of the electrolyte 1 M NaTFSI in 2G after different times of exposure to Na metal.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The results show that the spectra are dominated by diglyme, which shows intense bands in the range of 800–1500 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Clear differences between the samples are found at lower wavenumbers, where, depending on the salt, some characteristic bands appear. In the case of NaTFSI, these are especially notable in the range of 450–650 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Although electrolyte degradation was clearly visible by the naked eye (see images in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4 and S5</a>), the time-dependent measurements did not provide any indication for decomposition of the solvent or the salt. Overall, the results show that, for the above-mentioned reasons, the use of FTIR spectroscopy in our study is less straightforward for studying the stability of the electrolyte in contact with sodium metal.</div><div class="NLM_p">To detect possible crystalline decomposition products, XRD measurements of the sodium electrodes were done after exposing them for 3 weeks to the different electrolyte solutions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>). The sodium metal pieces were placed on a Cu foil as internal reference. While the XRD patterns in the case of NaOTf, NaPF<sub>6</sub>, and NaFSI show only very minor or no signs for decomposition products, they can be seen in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub> (formation of Na<sub>2</sub>O and little NaCl) and NaTFSI (formation of NaF). Especially for the latter salt, the reflex intensity of NaF is quite high, which is again a strong hint on that the use of NaTFSI leads to strong side reactions with sodium. From OEMS and XRD data, the major decomposition products are CH<sub>4</sub> and NaF, showing that both the solvent and the salt are decomposing.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Diffraction patterns of sodium metal after storage in different electrolyte solutions for 3 weeks. Copper foil was used as internal reference to correct for possible data shift. Stars, squares, and downward triangles represent NaCl, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and NaF respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Side Reactions with Graphite</h3><div class="NLM_p">As initial characterization, we recorded XRD patterns of the graphite electrodes after the fifth cycle, i.e., the electrode in its desodiated state. The XRD pattern provides an easy access to the crystallite dimensions of the graphite that are often expressed using <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>, describing the stack height of the graphenes (direction perpendicular to the basal plane of graphite, 00<i>l</i> diffraction lines) and the layer size <i>L</i><sub>a</sub>, describing the size of the graphene layers (<i>hk</i>0 diffraction lines). Results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>. Next to the (002) reflex of graphite at around 2θ = 26°, the diffraction pattern also shows signals from the copper current collector.
