The graphite <i>hk</i>0 diffraction lines are hardly visible due to preferential orientation of the graphite particles. In our earlier study (using NaOTf in diglyme as electrolyte solution), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> we showed that the intensity of the (002) reflex decreases significantly due to an activation process that largely occurs in the first cycle only. This activation includes exfoliation of larger graphite crystallites to thinner ones in the nanometer range. It is important to note that delamination, i.e., separation of the graphene sheets along with complete loss in crystallinity, is not observed. The exfoliated graphite remains crystalline, which is one of the important reasons for the excellent cycle life.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Diffraction patterns of powder graphite after five cycles (desodiated state) for the different salts. The stars indicate reflexes from copper foil that was used as internal reference. The strong reflex at around 2θ = 26°, enlarged in (b), indicates the graphene interlayer spacing of graphite (<i>d</i> = 3.35 Å).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">It is therefore expected that the (002) reflex is weaker compared to bulk graphite (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b). The loss in intensity, however, depends on the salt. The intensity of the (002) reflex after cycling is strongest for NaPF<sub>6</sub>, followed by NaClO<sub>4</sub> and NaOTf, indicating different degrees in exfoliation with NaPF<sub>6</sub> maintaining the largest stack height <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>. An even stronger decrease in intensity is found for NaFSI and NaTFSI, but here also the reflex position shifts to slightly larger values. Next to exfoliation, this also indicates a loss in crystalline order (or even delamination), which might be the result of continuous side reactions of NaFSI or NaTFSI containing electrolyte solutions with the graphite. The full width at half-maximum and an estimate for the <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> values using the Scherrer equation are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, Supporting Information. Note that the <i>hk</i>0 diffraction lines were too weak to apply the Scherrer equation. After activation, the crystallite size is about 36 nm in the case of NaOTf, whereas it is about 24 nm for NaTFSI, i.e., exfoliation is more severe in the case of NaTFSI. A long-term study was done for the NaOTf electrolyte (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>). The capacity retention of 95.6% over 2000 cycles combined with a very stable voltage profile clearly confirms the structural stability, i.e., the graphite lattice is maintained. Loss of crystallinity, e.g., induced by high-energy ball milling, would prevent the solvent co-intercalation reaction, leading to a different voltage profile resembling that of soft carbon (see ref  <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a>). However, the average crystallite dimensions seem to decrease gradually, i.e., the exfoliation continues at a very low rate (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13c</a>). Applying the Scherrer equation to the XRD data of graphite cycled 2000 times, a crystal size of about 10–15 nm can be estimated. The poor behavior for NaTFSI can be also seen from the XRD patterns of sodiated graphite electrodes (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">For further analysis by SEM and TEM/electron diffraction (ED), we largely restrict our comparison to the salts with the best (NaOTf) and worst (NaTFSI) performance. SEM cross sections of the electrodes after five cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>. Before cycling, the electrode thickness was about 45 μm in both cases. After cycling, the electrode thickness increased to 65 μm (NaOTf) and 145 μm (NaTFSI). An increase in thickness compared to the starting value was expected based on our previous findings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> The increase for NaTFSI is, however, much more extreme, which is another strong hint on structural degradation (or delamination) of the graphite when using this salt. A more detailed discussion on the thickness change is given below along with results from in situ electrochemical dilatometry.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. SEM cross-sectional images of graphite electrodes after five cycles (desodiated state) in electrolyte solutions of (a) 1 M NaOTf in diglyme and (b) 1 M NaTFSI in diglyme. The copper current collector at the bottom has a thickness of 15 μm. Electrodes contain 10 wt % poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) as binder.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For TEM/selected area ED (SAED) measurements, graphite powders instead of graphite electrodes were used to ease the sampling and to avoid artifacts from the binder. Results for NaOTf and NaTFSI are compared in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>. Overall, it was much easier to detect ordered graphite domains for the sample cycled with NaOTf. Rings in the SAEDs of NaOTf could be indexed with the graphite crystal structure. In contrast, the use of NaTFSI leads to more disorder in the structure along with several other crystalline decomposition products. Note that the quality of the SAED pattern was not sufficient to resolve the nature of the decomposition products.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. TEM and HRTEM images of the graphite particles after five cycles (desodiated state) in different electrolyte solutions of (a, b) 1 M NaOTf in diglyme and (c, d) 1 M NaTFSI in diglyme. Insets: SAED patterns for areas in (a) and (b); the semicircles indicate the expected positions for the graphene interlayer spacing of graphite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) analysis showed that the samples cycled with NaTFSI contained larger amounts of elements stemming from the salt. Signals for Na, S, F, and O were much larger compared to when NaOTf was used (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). This indicates irreversible trapping of these elements within the disordered carbon structure as a result of side reactions.</div><div class="NLM_p">We further applied in situ electrochemical dilatometry to compare the dynamic behavior of the graphite electrodes during cycling in either NaOTf or NaTFSI containing electrolyte solutions (see <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>a,b). Results for NaOTf were again in line with our previous findings, i.e., after the activation cycle, the change in electrode thickness within one cycle (“breathing”) is quite constant. The breathing amounts to about 30 μm along with a slight shrinkage of the electrode upon cycling. In the case of NaTFSI, the behavior is notably different. The shrinkage during desodiation is always much smaller compared to the increase during sodiation. The thickness of the electrode increases 3-fold within a few cycles reaching about 150 μm (desodiated state). This is well in line with the SEM results on the electrode cross sections shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>. The fourth cycle is magnified in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>b. Although the shrinkage in electrode thickness during desodiation is similar for both salts, the electrode expansion during sodiation is much larger in the case of NaTFSI. This points on an insufficient reductive stability of this electrolyte. The poor performance for the NaTFSI electrolyte is therefore directly reflected by a poor structural stability of the electrode. Finally, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">10</a>c shows the results for the two best-performing salts (NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in comparison. The breathing behavior is nearly identical, indicating that both salts are much more suitable (the shift in capacity is due to intrinsic limitations of the dilatometer cell, which sometimes leads to slightly nonideal utilization of the electrode). Note that dilatometry has been recently also applied by Karimi et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> who studied the influence of the type of glyme (with NaOTf as salt) on the electrode dynamics of the same reaction. Compared to their results, one can see that the salt can have an even stronger influence on the electrode dynamics than the type of glyme.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. Results from in situ electrochemical dilatometry measurements using NaOTf or NaTFSI as conductive salt (a) thickness of the electrodes over the first four cycles and (b) comparison of the fourth cycle. (c) Comparison over five cycles for NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>. The measurements were performed at C/10 = 11 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.5 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. The initial electrode thickness was around 45 μm.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Finally, we applied OEMS in sodium|graphite cells to monitor the gas release online. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a> shows the evolution of H<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>, CO, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> for the different salts during five consecutive voltammetric cycles, and the cumulative amounts can be found in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">12</a>. The gas evolution profile and the integrated gas amount for H<sub>2</sub> show a very strong release in the case of NaFSI. The total amount of H<sub>2</sub> released is over 30 times larger than for the other electrolytes. Two gas-releasing processes can be easily distinguished: The first one occurs already at <i>t</i> = 0 h during the constant-voltage step (green area in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a>). The signal decays exponentially. As the graphite is unsodiated at this stage, this gas evolution peak indicates side reactions at the Na counter electrode, likely caused by residual impurities (note that this behavior was not observed for Na|Na cells). As the voltage of the cell is lowered, further H<sub>2</sub> peaks are observed on top of the decaying evolution profile. The most significant are found below 0.8 V and are observed for each cycle. Along with that, strong CO evolution takes place below 0.8 V as well as minor release of C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> and CH<sub>4</sub>. Overall, this verifies reductive decomposition of the electrolyte on the graphite electrode. H<sub>2</sub>, CO, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> evolution, even though at a lower rate, are observed also in the following cycles and are indicating a continuous electrolyte decomposition.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. (a) H<sub>2</sub>, (b) CH<sub>4</sub>, (c) CO, and (d) C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> gas evolution for Na/graphite cells over five voltammetric cycles (<i>v</i> = 55 μV s<sup>–1</sup>) for the different electrolyte solutions. Before cycling, the cell was equilibrated at 2.2 V for 4 h (green area).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig12" data-index="12" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 12</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/medium/am9b06760_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0012.jpeg" id="gr12" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 12. Total gas amounts for H<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>, CO, and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> evolution during five cyclovoltammetric cycles (<i>v</i> = 55 μV s<sup>–1</sup>) with respect to the measured electrolyte solution.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/asset/images/large/am9b06760_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b06760&amp;id=fig12"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Significant CO evolution can also be observed at voltages below 0.8 V for the NaTFSI system. In addition, strong evolutions of CH<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> are observed. For all gases, several common features can be identified: All three gases are clearly observed during the constant-voltage phases, which is in agreement with the observations from the Na|Na cells and shows the instability of this electrolyte toward Na metal. When decreasing the voltage below 1.05 V in the first cycle, further evolution of CH<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> along with slight evolution of CO takes place, altogether indicating reductive decomposition of the electrolyte on the graphite electrode. A further contribution to the gas release can be observed during the oxidation potential sweep when the graphite electrode is desodiated and fresh Na is plated on the Na counter electrode. In line with the observations from the Na|Na cells, this indicates reactions between the electrolyte and the freshly plated sodium. Here, however, additional release of CH<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> takes place (see peaks marked with an asterisk (*) in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">11</a>b,d).</div><div class="NLM_p">In the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the evolution of H<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>, and CO is significantly lower than that for NaFSI and NaTFSI. The only strong gas release is observed for C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>, for which a continuous evolution is observed over the whole experiment. This can be taken as a clear sign for a continuous decomposition of the electrolyte, probably at the sodium counter electrode. On top of that, weak peaks are observed close to the potential minimum, most likely stemming from reactions with the sodiated graphite.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In contrast to these three salts, gas release in the case of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf is much smaller and mostly restricted to the first cycle. As the electrochemical performance of the cells is also best when using NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf, it becomes obvious that the reversibility of the co-intercalation reaction is clearly linked to the conductive salt anion.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Summary and Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The stability of diglyme-based electrolytes with different conductive salts has been studied with respect to graphite (solvent co-intercalation reaction) and sodium metal (plating/stripping processes). While the solvent co-intercalation reaction is a highly reversible process for, e.g., NaOTf, cycle life is poor in the case of NaTFSI and NaFSI. Several analytical tools were used to study the stability of the different electrolytes. Overall, the following conclusions can be made.</div><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Sodium Electrode</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Plating/stripping tests in symmetrical Na|Na cells show the highest resistance for NaFSI and the lowest for NaOTf, followed by NaPF<sub>6</sub>. For the latter salts, the polarization remains constant during cycling, indicating a fairly stable interface. For NaTFSI and NaClO<sub>4</sub>, the resistance increases over cycling, indicating an instable interface leading to decomposition reactions. XRD evidences NaF as a major solid decomposition product in the case of NaTFSI and Na<sub>2</sub>O in the case of NaClO<sub>4</sub>. OEMS shows that larger amounts of CH<sub>4</sub> are released in the case of NaTFSI. The overall total gas release was smallest for NaOTf, NaFSI, and NaPF<sub>6</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Graphite Electrode</h3><div class="NLM_p">Intercalation of solvated ions into graphite is accompanied by very notable irreversible reactions in the case of NaTFSI and NaFSI. The co-intercalation reaction leads to exfoliation, the degree depending on the type of salt. The exfoliation tendency is smallest for NaPF<sub>6</sub> and by far the largest for NaFSI and NaTFSI. For the latter, even delamination and complete structural disintegration may take place. This is further corroborated by TEM showing a loss in crystallinity in the case of NaTFSI, while crystalline domains are easily detectable for NaOTf. SEM cross-sectional measurements show an excessive growth in electrode thickness in the case of NaTFSI. These observations are clearly supported by dilatometry, which shows a continuous growth of the electrode during cycling. OEMS shows that most gas is released in the case of NaFSI and NaTFSI, while the least gas release takes place for NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Overall, our results clearly show that NaTFSI and NaFSI lead to severe side reactions when used in Na|Na or Na|graphite cells. Considering graphite, we cannot exclude a cross-talk effect from the sodium counter electrode, but as the electrochemical studies show side reactions with the graphite electrode starting from the very beginning, it is quite safe to assume that these salts are unsuitable for graphite electrodes in sodium-ion batteries. Based on our analysis, we can rank the suitability of the different salts in the following order: NaOTf (most suitable) ≥ NaPF<sub>6</sub> &gt; NaClO<sub>4</sub> &gt; NaFSI ≫ NaTFSI (least suitable). It remains, however, an intriguing question why NaTFSI is obviously so incompatible with sodium metal electrodes while the combination of LiTFSI and lithium does not show any comparable problems.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The results of this study also explain the only seeming contradiction between our previous study <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> finding an SEI-free interface on the graphite surface (using NaOTf as salt) and Maibach et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> who found an SEI (when using NaFSI as salt).</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">4.1.  Electrode Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrodes contained 90% graphite (MTI Corp., mesocarbon microbeads graphite powder for Li-ion battery anode) and 10% binder [poly(vinylidene fluoride) “EQ-Lib-PVDF”, MTI Corp.]. Slurries were mixed in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (Sigma-Aldrich) and cast onto dendritic copper foil. The average loading of electrodes after drying (12 h at 120 °C) was 2–3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">4.2.  Electrochemical (in Situ) Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Cells for galvanostatic cycling were prepared in an argon-filled glovebox (&lt;0.1 ppm O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O) with electrodes of 12 mm in diameter. For galvanostatic cycling, coin cells (CR2032 Coin Cells by MTI Corp.) and Swagelok-type cells were used. Sodium metal (BASF SE) was used as counter and reference electrodes. Whatman glass microfiber filters (GF/A) were used as separator and were soaked in 100 μL of electrolyte solution. For the electrolyte preparation, sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf, purity &gt;98.0%, Sigma-Aldrich), sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>, purity &gt;99%, Alfa Aesar), sodium perchlorate, (NaClO<sub>4</sub>, purity &gt;97%, Alfa Aesar), sodium(I) bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (NaTFSI, purity 99.5%, Solvionic), and sodium(I) bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI, purity 99.7%, Solvionic) were predried overnight at 100 °C in a Büchi oven. Diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (diglyme, 2G) was purchased from Sigma-Aldrich and dried overnight using molecular sieves (4 Å). The electrolyte concentration was 1 M in all cases. The coin cells were prepared at 1000 psi using a manual crimper (TG-110, Targray). Electrochemical measurements were carried out at room temperature using a Biologic BCS battery cycler between 0.01 and 2.5 V. Measurements were performed galvanostatically at different current densities. The C rate was calculated assuming a theoretical capacity of <i>q</i><sub>th</sub> = 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to Na(diglyme)<sub>2</sub>C<sub>20</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Therefore, 1 C corresponds to a specific current of <i>i</i> = 110 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and experiments were done at C/3 and C/10. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed using instruments from Biologic. Information on the OEMS setup can be found elsewhere <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">4.3.  Analytical Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p">SEM images were obtained using a benchtop SEM machine (Phenom ProX) operating at 15 kV accelerating voltage. Electrodes were removed from cells and analyzed without further washing. For cross-sectional measurements, electrodes were cut by a knife. Air exposure during sample transfer was minimized to about 60 s at maximum. A digital thickness dial gauge from Käfer Messuhrenfabrik GmbH was used to determine the electrode thickness. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded with a Bruker D2 Phaser instrument using Cu Kα radiation and an airtight sample holder. For XRD measurements, electrodes without binder were prepared to minimize noise. The powders were placed on Cu foil as internal reference.</div><div class="NLM_p last">TEM and SAED were carried out using an FEI Tecnai G2 FEG machine operating at 200 kV. The samples were deposited on a copper grid with a holey carbon support film and then transferred to the microscope. The samples were prepared and mounted on the TEM holder inside an Ar glovebox.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b06760" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b06760</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Table summarizing results from several related experimental studies, additional electrochemical measurements (long-term cycling, rate capability tests, EIS), photographs of sodium exposed to different electrolyte solutions, additional OEMS data, XRD patterns, and EDX results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b06760/suppl_file/am9b06760_si_001.pdf">am9b06760_si_001.pdf (2.86 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b06760" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research on electrochem. Na intercalation in battery system has been reported since the early 1980s but Na-ion batteries are not commercialized so far though studies on Li-ion batteries have been reported since the late 1970s and the practical batteries have been extensively utilized for portable device applications in the world since 1991.  Now, targeted application of research and development for rechargeable batteries has changed toward realization of the sustainable energy society.  With the change in social situation and development of the battery technol., studies on Na-ion batteries have been attracted significant interests since 2010.  Although research interests of the electrode materials for Na-ion batteries are evoked in many researchers, advantages, disadvantages, and issues are not fully discussed for realizing the commercialization of Na-ion batteries.  In this article, practical issues and perspective are reviewed on the basis of mainly authors exptl. experiences, know-how, and results, and the future direction is proposed to overcome the issues and to challenge the advanced performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700463"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Muñoz-Márquez, M.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From Lithium-Ion to Sodium-Ion Batteries: Advantages, Challenges, and Surprises</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Nayak, Prasant Kumar; Yang, Liangtao; Brehm, Wolfgang; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mobile and stationary energy storage by rechargeable batteries is a topic of broad societal and economical relevance.  Lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. is at the forefront of the development, but a massively growing market will likely put severe pressure on resources and supply chains.  Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been reconsidered with the aim of providing a lower-cost alternative that is less susceptible to resource and supply risks.  On paper, the replacement of lithium by sodium in a battery seems straightforward at first, but unpredictable surprises are often found in practice.  What happens when replacing lithium by sodium in electrode reactions.  This review provides a state-of-the art overview on the redox behavior of materials when used as electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, resp.  Advantages and challenges related to the use of sodium instead of lithium are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1007/s41061-017-0168-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Medenbach, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cell Concepts of Metal-Sulfur Batteries (Metal = Li, Na, K, Mg): Strategies for Using Sulfur in Energy Storage Applications</span>. <i>Top.                 10.1007/s41061-017-0168-x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D375%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D81%26journal%3DTop.%2BCurr.%2BChem.%26author%3DL.%2BMedenbach%26author%3DP.%2BAdelhelm%26title%3DCell%2BConcepts%2Bof%2BMetal-Sulfur%2BBatteries%2B%2528Metal%2B%253D%2BLi%252C%2BNa%252C%2BK%252C%2BMg%2529%253A%2BStrategies%2Bfor%2BUsing%2BSulfur%2Bin%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BApplications%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs41061-017-0168-x&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b06760&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs41061-017-0168-x&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cell Concepts of Metal-Sulfur Batteries (Metal = Li, Na, K, Mg): Strategies for Using Sulfur in Energy Storage Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Medenbach Lukas; Adelhelm Philipp; Medenbach Lukas; Adelhelm Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Topics in current chemistry (Cham)</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">81</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">There is great interest in using sulfur as active component in rechargeable batteries thanks to its low cost and high specific charge (1672 mAh/g).  The electrochemistry of sulfur, however, is complex and cell concepts are required, which differ from conventional designs.  This review summarizes different strategies for utilizing sulfur in rechargeable batteries among membrane concepts, polysulfide concepts, all-solid-state concepts as well as high-temperature systems.  Among the more popular lithium-sulfur and sodium-sulfur batteries, we also comment on recent results on potassium-sulfur and magnesium-sulfur batteries.  Moreover, specific properties related to the type of light metal are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.3762/bjnano.6.105"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hartmann, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bender, C.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From lithium to sodium: cell chemistry of room temperature sodium-air and sodium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Adelhelm, Philipp; Hartmann, Pascal; Bender, Conrad L.; Busche, Martin; Eufinger, Christine; Janek, Juergen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Beilstein Journal of Nanotechnology</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Beilstein-Institut zur Foerderung der Chemischen Wissenschaften</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research devoted to room temp. lithium-sulfur (Li/S8) and lithium-oxygen (Li/O2) batteries has significantly increased over the past ten years.  The race to develop such cell systems is mainly motivated by the very high theor. energy d. and the abundance of sulfur and oxygen.  The cell chem., however, is complex, and progress toward practical device development remains hampered by some fundamental key issues, which are currently being tackled by numerous approaches.  Quite surprisingly, not much is known about the analogous sodium-based battery systems, although the already commercialized, high-temp. Na/S8 and Na/NiCl2 batteries suggest that a rechargeable battery based on sodium is feasible on a large scale.  Moreover, the natural abundance of sodium is an attractive benefit for the development of batteries based on low cost components.  This review provides a summary of the state-of-the-art knowledge on lithium-sulfur and lithium-oxygen batteries and a direct comparison with the analogous sodium systems.  The general properties, major benefits and challenges, recent strategies for performance improvements and general guidelines for further development are summarized and critically discussed.  In general, the substitution of lithium for sodium has a strong impact on the overall properties of the cell reaction and differences in ion transport, phase stability, electrode potential, energy d., etc. can be thus expected.  Whether these differences will benefit a more reversible cell chem. is still an open question, but some of the first reports on room temp. Na/S8 and Na/O2 cells already show some exciting differences as compared to the established Li/S8 and Li/O2 systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00162J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Titirici, M.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Intercalation chemistry of graphite: alkali metal ions and beyond</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Use of Graphite as a Highly Reversible Electrode with Superior Cycle Life for Sodium-Ion Batteries by Making Use of Co-Intercalation Phenomena</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jache, Birte; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although being the std. anode material in Li-ion batteries (LIBs), graphite so far is considered to fail application in Na-ion batteries (NIBs) because the Na-C system lacks suitable binary intercalation compds.  This limitation can be circumvented by using co-intercalation phenomena in a diglyme-based electrolyte.  The resulting compd. is a stage-I ternary intercalation compd. with an estd. stoichiometry of Na(diglyme)2C20.  Highlights of the electrode reaction are its high energy efficiency, the small irreversible loss during the 1st cycle, and a superior cycle life with capacities close to 100 mA-h g-1 for 1000 cycles and coulomb efficiencies &gt;99.87%.  A 1-to-one comparison with the analog Li-based cell shows that the Na-based system performs better and also withstands higher currents.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402984"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, Y.-U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Storage Behavior in Natural Graphite using Ether-based Electrolyte Systems</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Storage Behavior in Natural Graphite using Ether-based Electrolyte Systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Haegyeom; Hong, Jihyun; Park, Young-Uk; Kim, Jinsoo; Hwang, Insang; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">534-541</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This work reports that natural graphite is capable of Na insertion and extn. with a remarkable reversibility using ether-based electrolytes.  Natural graphite (the most well-known anode material for Li-ion batteries) has been barely studied as a suitable anode for Na rechargeable batteries due to the lack of Na intercalation capability.  Herein, graphite is not only capable of Na intercalation but also exhibits outstanding performance as an anode for Na-ion batteries.  The graphite anode delivers a reversible capacity of ≈150 mA-h/g with a cycle stability for 2500 cycles, and more than 75 mA-h/g at 10 A/g despite its micrometer-size (≈100 μm).  An Na storage mechanism in graphite, where Na+-solvent co-intercalation occurs combined with partial pseudocapacitive behaviors, is shown.  It is demonstrated that the electrolyte solvent species significantly affect the electrochem. properties, not only rate capability but also redox potential.  The feasibility of graphite in a Na full cell is also confirmed in conjunction with the Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7 cathode, delivering an energy of ≈120 W-h/kg while maintaining ≈70% of the initial capacity after 250 cycles.  This exceptional behavior of natural graphite promises new avenues for the development of cost-effective and reliable Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/C6CP00651E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jache, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Binder, J.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comparative study on the impact of different glymes and their derivatives as electrolyte solvents for graphite co-intercalation electrodes in lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jache, Birte; Binder, Jan Oliver; Abe, Takeshi; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">18</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">14299-14316</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The abundance of sodium has recently sparked considerable interest in sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  Their similarity to conventional lithium-ion technol. is obvious; however, the cell chem. often significantly deviates.  Graphite, although being the std. neg. electrode in Li-ion batteries, is largely inactive for Na-ion storage in conventional non-aq. carbonate-based electrolytes, for example.  Very recently, it has been demonstrated that graphite can be activated for Na-ion storage in cells with ether-based electrolytes.  The storage mechanism is based on co-intercalation of solvent mols. along with the Na-ions, forming ternary graphite intercalation compds. (t-GICs).  This process is highly reversible but yet poorly understood.  Here, a comprehensive study is provided on the formation and the stability of t-GICs.  A series of ether solvents are being discussed: linear glymes with different chain lengths (mono-, di-, tri-, and tetraglyme), several derivs. with side groups as well as THF as a cyclic ether and one crown ether.  It is shown that the redox potentials shift depending on the ether chain length and mixing of ethers might enable tailoring of the redox behavior.  The inferior behavior of triglyme is likely due to the less ideal ion coordination.  Complementary expts. with lithium are made and demonstrate the superior behavior of sodium.  It was found that the increase in graphene layer spacing during intercalation only slightly depends on the chain length and is in the range of 250%, and still mech. stability is preserved.  It is further shown that the t-GICs possess chem. stability and demonstrated that the kinetically favored charge transfer is probably due to the absence of a solid electrolyte interphase.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE02051D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, M.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, W.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium intercalation chemistry in graphite</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium intercalation chemistry in graphite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Haegyeom; Hong, Jihyun; Yoon, Gabin; Kim, Hyunchul; Park, Kyu-Young; Park, Min-Sik; Yoon, Won-Sub; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The insertion of guest species in graphite is the key feature utilized in applications ranging from energy storage and liq. purifn. to the synthesis of graphene.  Recently, it was discovered that solvated-Na-ion intercalation can occur in graphite even though the insertion of Na ions alone is thermodynamically impossible; this phenomenon enables graphite to function as a promising anode for Na-ion batteries.  In an effort to understand this unusual behavior, we investigate the solvated-Na-ion intercalation mechanism using in operando X-ray diffraction anal., electrochem. titrn., real-time optical observation, and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns.  The ultrafast intercalation is demonstrated in real time using millimeter-sized highly ordered pyrolytic graphite, in which instantaneous insertion of solvated-Na-ions occurs (in less than 2 s).  The formation of various stagings with solvated-Na-ions in graphite is obsd. and precisely quantified for the first time.  The atomistic configuration of the solvated-Na-ions in graphite is proposed based on the exptl. results and DFT calcns.  The correlation between the properties of various solvents and the Na ion co-intercalation further suggests a strategy to tune the electrochem. performance of graphite electrodes in Na rechargeable batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.8b07915"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goktas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Akduman, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balducci, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Temperature-Induced Activation of Graphite Co-intercalation Reactions for Glymes and Crown Ethers in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Temperature-Induced Activation of Graphite Co-intercalation Reactions for Glymes and Crown Ethers in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goktas, Mustafa; Akduman, Baris; Huang, Peihua; Balducci, Andrea; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The intercalation of solvated ions into graphite leads to ternary graphite intercalation compds. (t-GICs).  Here, the authors study the impact of temp. on the electrochem. activity of graphite electrodes for co-intercalation reactions between 20 and 80° in Na cells.  For this, a range of linear ethers (mono-, di-, tri-, and tetraglyme) were studied.  For the 1st time, pentaglyme and several crown ethers are also studied.  Several solvents that appear as unsuitable for the co-intercalation reaction at room temp. provide higher capacities at elevated temps.  The most evident example is pentaglyme.  While poor performance is found at room temp. for this solvent (20 mAhg-1 and large polarization), almost complete use (∼110 mAh g-1) and excellent rate capability were obtained at 45°.  The reactions involving mono-, di-, tetra-, and (at elevated temps. also) pentaglyme are thermodynamically controlled, whereas the reaction with triglyme is kinetically limited.  Also the crown ether 18c6 can be used as an electrolyte solvent above its m.p., which, for the 1st time, demonstrates the synthesis of t-GICs with crown ethers by electrochem. methods.  Addnl., parasitic reactions appear at elevated temps.  Overall, new co-intercalation reactions can be activated by temp.  The chem. of these compds. might therefore be much richer than previously thought.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b04187"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cohn, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A max. sodium capacity of ∼35 mAh/g has hampered the use of cryst. carbon nanostructures for sodium ion battery anodes.  We demonstrate that a diglyme solvent shell encapsulating a sodium ion acts as a "nonstick" coating to facilitate rapid ion insertion into cryst. few-layer graphene and bypass slow desolvation kinetics.  This yields storage capacities above 150 mAh/g, cycling performance with negligible capacity fade over 8000 cycles, and ∼100 mAh/g capacities maintained at currents of 30 A/g (∼12 s charge).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion battery is a potential sustainable energy storage system due to its abundance and low cost.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), in view of the large availability and low cost of sodium raw materials, represent an important class of electrochem. systems suitable for application in large-scale energy storage.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NMR-Detected Dynamics of Sodium Co-Intercalation with Diglyme Solvent Molecules in Graphite Anodes Linked to Prolonged Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Leifer, Nicole; Greenstein, Miryam Fayena; Mor, Albert; Aurbach, Doron; Goobes, Gil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Following the success of Li-ion batteries, Na-ion batteries are becoming an important economical alternative, particularly where wt. and d. considerations are not of primary importance.  Graphite, the anode of choice for nearly all com. Li-ion battery applications, has only recently been successfully used as such in Na systems.  This unprecedented success was due to the proper choice of solvent, e.g., diglyme.  Lithium performs poorly under such conditions, which is the converse of their resp. behavior in std. carbonate solvents.  These phenomena were attributed to co-intercalation of the alkali ions upon their complexation with the smaller solvent mols.  In the case of Li, the use of such solvents leads to deterioration, while in the case of Na, it improves its electrochem. performance so substantially as to make the previously irrelevant Na-graphite system viable.  Several studies have since followed, mainly focusing on the Na-diglyme intercalation; however, a thorough understanding of the mechanisms of ternary intercalation into graphite for both Na and Li is still lacking.  In particular, the characteristic differences in location and dynamics of the guest complexes in the host material upon electrochem. cycling are not yet fully understood.  The co-intercalation mechanisms of Na and Li in diglyme into graphite were explored via solid-state NMR spectroscopy.  Direct evidence for the at. proximity of both Na-(diglyme)2 and Li-(diglyme)2 complexes to the graphite planes in discharged electrodes was obsd.  Reduced mobility and stronger coupling of the Li-(diglyme)2 complex to the graphene electrons are seen, whereas higher mobility and weaker coupling to the host are detected for the Na-(diglyme)2 complexes in the galleries, providing mol. cues for the difference in cycling performance of the two systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2017.03.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, S.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Origin of excellent rate and cycle performance of Na+-solvent cointercalated graphite vs. poor performance of Li+-solvent case</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jung, Sung Chul; Kang, Yong-Ju; Han, Young-Kyu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">34</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">456-462</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Despite its high reversibility for Li+ intercalation, graphite is known to be electrochem. inactive for Na+ intercalation.  On the contrary, recent studies have demonstrated that graphite is active and shows excellent rate and cycle performance for Na+-solvent cointercalation but it exhibits poor performance for Li+-solvent cointercalation.  Herein, we elucidate the mechanism of Li+- and Na+-solvent cointercalation into graphite and the origin of the strikingly different electrochem. performance of Li+- and Na+-solvent cointercalation cells.  Na+ intercalation into graphite is thermodynamically unfavorable, but Na+-diglyme cointercalation is very favorable.  The diglyme-graphene van der Waals interaction reinforces the interlayer coupling strength and thereby improves the resistance of graphite to exfoliation.  The transport of solvated Na ions is so fast that the diffusivity of Na+-diglyme complexes is markedly faster (by five orders of magnitude) than that of Li+-diglyme complexes.  The very fast Na+-diglyme cond. is attributed to facile sliding of flat diglyme mols., which completely solvate Na ions in the interlayer space of graphite.  The slow Li+-diglyme cond. is ascribed to steric hindrance to codiffusion caused by bent diglyme mols. that incompletely solvate Li ions.  The bent and flat diglyme mols. surrounding Li and Na ions, resp., are highly assocd. with the strong Li+-graphene and weak Na+-graphene interactions, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702724"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goktas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bolli, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Berg, E.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Surface Layer Evolution on Graphite During Electrochemical Sodium-tetraglyme Co-intercalation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Maibach, Julia; Jeschull, Fabian; Brandell, Daniel; Edstroem, Kristina; Valvo, Mario</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12373-12381</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">One obstacle in sodium ion batteries is the lack of suitable anode materials.  As recently shown, the most common anode material of the state of the art lithium ion batteries, graphite, can be used for sodium ion storage as well, if ether-based electrolyte solvents are used.  These solvents cointercalate with the sodium ions leading to the highly reversible formation of ternary graphite intercalation compds. (t-GIC).  In order for the solvent cointercalation to work efficiently, it is expected that only a very thin surface layer forms during electrochem. cycling.  In this article, we therefore present the first dedicated study of the surface layer evolution on t-GICs using soft XPS.  This technique with its inherent high surface sensitivity and low probing depth is an ideal tool to study the underlying interfacial reactions during the sodiation and desodiation of graphite.  In this report, we apply this approach to graphite composite electrodes cycled in Na half cells with a 1 M sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide/tetraethylene glycol di-Me ether (NaFSI/TEG-DME) electrolyte.  We have found a surface layer on the cycled electrodes, mainly composed of salt decompn. products and hydrocarbons, in line with irreversible capacity losses obsd. in the electrochem. cycling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Converting 'trash' waste residua to active functional materials 'treasure' with high added value is being regarded as a promising way to achieve the sustainable energy demands.  Carbonaceous materials cannot insert sodium except when graphite co-intercalates solvents such as diglyme.  Here, we show that petroleum coke and shale coke annealed at different temps. can also insert sodium by reversible intercalation phenomena in a diglyme-based electrolyte.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) are currently being considered for large-scale energy storage.  Optimization of SIB electrolytes is, however, still largely lacking.  Here the authors exhaustively evaluate NaPF6 in diglyme as an electrolyte of choice - both physicochem. properties and extensive electrochem. tests including half as well as full cells.  Fundamentally, the ionic cond. is quite comparable to carbonate based electrolytes and obeying the fractional Walden rule with viscosity.  Na metal to work well as a ref. electrode and the electrochem. stability, evaluated potentiostatically for various electrodes and corroborated by DFT calcns., to be satisfactory in the entire voltage range of 0-4.4 V were found.  Galvanostatic cycling at C/10 of half and full cells using Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) or Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) as cathodes and hard C (Hc) as anodes indicate rapid capacity fading in cells with Hc anodes, possibly originating in a lack of a stable SEI or by trapping of Na.  Aiming to understand this capacity fade further, the authors conducted a GC/MS anal. to det. electrolyte redn. products and to propose redn. pathways, concluding that oligomer and/or alkoxide formation is possible.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Glyme-based nonaqueous electrolytes for rechargeable lithium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tobishima, S.; Morimoto, H.; Aoki, M.; Saito, Y.; Inose, T.; Fukumoto, T.; Kuryu, T.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Poly(ethylene glycol)dimethyl ethers [(MeO(CH2CH2O)nMe, n = 1, 2, 3, and 4)] are generally known as glymes.  The cond., Li-ion solvation and charge-discharge cycling efficiency of Li anodes in glyme-based electrolytes for rechargeable Li cells was studied with 1M LiPF6 as the solute.  The properties of the glymes were studied by using a ternary mixed solvent consisting of n-glyme, ethylene carbonate (EC) and methylethylcarbonate (MEC).  This was because the soly. of LiPF6 is &lt;1M in a single solvent consisting of a n-glyme.  The glyme solns. exhibited higher cond. and a higher Li cycling efficiency than EC/MEC.  The cond. increased with a decrease in ethylene oxide chain no., n, and soln. viscosity.  The decrease in soln. viscosity was due to the change in the Li-ion solvation structure that occurred when a glyme was added to EC/MEC.  The selective solvation of the glyme with respect to Li ions was clearly demonstrated by 13C-NMR measurements.  The Li cycling efficiency depended on the charge-discharge current, Ips.  When n increased there was an increase in Li cycling efficiency at a low Ips and a decrease in the redn. potential of the glymes.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Role of Electrolyte Anions in the Na-O2 Battery: Implications for NaO2 Solvation and the Stability of the Sodium Solid Electrolyte Interphase in Glyme Ethers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lutz, Lukas; Alves Dalla Corte, Daniel; Tang, Mingxue; Salager, Elodie; Deschamps, Michael; Grimaud, Alexis; Johnson, Lee; Bruce, Peter G.; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">29</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein we investigate the influence of the sodium salt anion on the performance of Na-O2 batteries.  To illustrate the solvent-solute interactions in various solvents, we use 23Na-NMR to probe the environment of Na+ in the presence of different anions (ClO4-, PF6-, OTf-, or TFSi-).  Strong solvation of either the Na+ or the anion leads to solvent-sepd. ions where the anion has no measurable impact on the Na+ chem. shift.  Contrarily, in weakly solvating solvents the increasing interaction of the anion (ClO4- &lt; PF6- &lt; OTf- &lt; TFSi-) can indeed stabilize the Na+ due to formation of contact ion pairs.  However, by employing these electrolytes in Na-O2 cells, we demonstrate that changing from weakly interacting anions (ClO4-) to TFSi does not result in elevated battery performance.  Nevertheless, a strong dependence of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) stability on the choice of sodium salt was found.  By correlation of the phys. properties of the electrolyte with the chem. SEI compn., the crucial role of the anion in the SEI formation process is revealed.  The remarkable differences and consequences for long-term stability are further established by cycling Na coin cells, where electrolytes using NaTFSi are absolutely detrimental for metallic sodium, employing NaOTF and NaClO4 leads to short-term stability, and only the combination of 1,2-dimethoxyethane with NaPF6 allows for high efficiency and performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00328"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seh, Z.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3312-3319</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHEMIZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1864-5631</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are promising energy-storage devices with advantages such as low cost and highly abundant raw materials.  To probe the electrochem. properties of NIBs, Na metal is most frequently applied as the ref. and/or counter electrode in state-of-the-art literature.  However, the high reactivity of the Na metal and its impact on the electrochem. performance is usually neglected.  Spontaneous reactions of Na metal with org. electrolytes and the importance of crit. interpretation of electrochem. expts. is emphasized.  When using Na-metal half-cells, decompn. products contaminate the electrolyte during the electrochem. measurement and can easily lead to wrong conclusions about the stability of the active materials.  The cycling stability is highly affected by these electrolyte contaminations, which is proven by comparing Na-metal-free cell with Na-metal-contg. cells.  A more stable cycling performance of the Li4Ti5O12 half-cells can be obsd. when replacing the Na metal counter and ref. electrodes with activated C electrodes.  This difference is attributed to the altered properties of the electrolyte as a result of contamination and to different surface chemistries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201606663"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goncharova, L. V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lushington, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yadegari, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superior Stable and Long Life Sodium Metal Anodes Achieved by Atomic Layer Deposition</span>. <i>Adv. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1149/2.0151514jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Review-Practical Issues and Future Perspective for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.
